Where it is available, it is prudent to assume only single bytes of OOB data will be reliably handled.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since the feature is not frequently used, it is not well tested on some platforms and has been associated with <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vulnerability (computing)">vulnerabilities</a>, <a href="/wiki/WinNuke" title="WinNuke">WinNuke</a> for instance.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Forcing_data_delivery">Forcing data delivery</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Forcing data delivery"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Normally, TCP waits for 200&#160;ms for a full packet of data to send (<a href="/wiki/Nagle%27s_Algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Nagle&#39;s Algorithm">Nagle's Algorithm</a> tries to group small messages into a single packet). This wait creates small, but potentially serious delays if repeated constantly during a file transfer. For example, a typical send block would be 4&#160;KB, a typical MSS is 1460, so 2 packets go out on a 10&#160;Mbit/s Ethernet taking ~1.2&#160;ms each followed by a third carrying the remaining 1176 after a 197&#160;ms pause because TCP is waiting for a full buffer. In the case of telnet, each user keystroke is echoed back by the server before the user can see it on the screen. This delay would become very annoying.
</p><p>Setting the <a href="/wiki/Network_socket" title="Network socket">socket</a> option <code>TCP_NODELAY</code> overrides the default 200&#160;ms send delay. Application programs use this socket option to force output to be sent after writing a character or line of characters.
</p><p>The <a href="#CITEREFRFC_793">RFC 793</a> defines the <code>PSH</code> push bit as "a message to the receiving TCP stack to send this data immediately up to the receiving application".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-comer&#95;15-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-comer-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is no way to indicate or control it in <a href="/wiki/User_space" class="mw-redirect" title="User space">user space</a> using <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_sockets" title="Berkeley sockets">Berkeley sockets</a>; it is controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Protocol_stack" title="Protocol stack">protocol stack</a> only.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Stevens2006&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevens2006-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Vulnerabilities">Vulnerabilities</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Vulnerabilities"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>TCP may be attacked in a variety of ways. The results of a thorough security assessment of TCP, along with possible mitigations for the identified issues, were published in 2009,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and was pursued within the <a href="/wiki/IETF" class="mw-redirect" title="IETF">IETF</a> through 2012.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Notable vulnerabilities include denial of service, connection hijacking, TCP veto and <a href="/wiki/TCP_reset_attack" title="TCP reset attack">TCP reset attack</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Denial_of_service">Denial of service</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Denial of service"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>By using a <a href="/wiki/IP_address_spoofing" title="IP address spoofing">spoofed IP address</a> and repeatedly sending <a href="/wiki/Mangled_packet" title="Mangled packet">purposely assembled</a> SYN packets, followed by many ACK packets, attackers can cause the server to consume large amounts of resources keeping track of the bogus connections. This is known as a <a href="/wiki/SYN_flood" title="SYN flood">SYN flood</a> attack. Proposed solutions to this problem include <a href="/wiki/SYN_cookies" title="SYN cookies">SYN cookies</a> and cryptographic puzzles, though SYN cookies come with their own set of vulnerabilities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sockstress" title="Sockstress">Sockstress</a> is a similar attack, that might be mitigated with system resource management.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An advanced DoS attack involving the exploitation of the TCP <i>persist timer</i> was analyzed in <a href="/wiki/Phrack" title="Phrack">Phrack</a> No. 66.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/PUSH_and_ACK_floods" title="PUSH and ACK floods">PUSH and ACK floods</a> are other variants.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Connection_hijacking">Connection hijacking</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Connection hijacking"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/TCP_sequence_prediction_attack" title="TCP sequence prediction attack">TCP sequence prediction attack</a></div>
<p>An attacker who is able to eavesdrop on a TCP session and redirect packets can hijack a TCP connection. To do so, the attacker learns the sequence number from the ongoing communication and forges a false segment that looks like the next segment in the stream. A simple hijack can result in one packet being erroneously accepted at one end. When the receiving host acknowledges the false segment, synchronization is lost.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hijacking may be combined with <a href="/wiki/ARP_spoofing" title="ARP spoofing">ARP spoofing</a> or other routing attacks that allow an attacker to take permanent control of the TCP connection.
</p><p>Impersonating a different IP address was not difficult prior to <a href="#CITEREFRFC_1948">RFC 1948</a> when the initial <i>sequence number</i> was easily guessable. The earlier implementations allowed an attacker to blindly send a sequence of packets that the receiver would believe came from a different IP address, without the need to intercept communication through ARP or routing attacks: it is enough to ensure that the legitimate host of the impersonated IP address is down, or bring it to that condition using <a href="/wiki/Denial-of-service_attack" title="Denial-of-service attack">denial-of-service attacks</a>. This is why the initial sequence number is now chosen at random.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="TCP_veto">TCP veto</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: TCP veto"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An attacker who can eavesdrop and predict the size of the next packet to be sent can cause the receiver to accept a malicious payload without disrupting the existing connection. The attacker injects a malicious packet with the sequence number and a payload size of the next expected packet. When the legitimate packet is ultimately received, it is found to have the same sequence number and length as a packet already received and is silently dropped as a normal duplicate packet—the legitimate packet is <i>vetoed</i> by the malicious packet. Unlike in connection hijacking, the connection is never desynchronized and communication continues as normal after the malicious payload is accepted. TCP veto gives the attacker less control over the communication but makes the attack particularly resistant to detection. The only evidence to the receiver that something is amiss is a single duplicate packet, a normal occurrence in an IP network. The sender of the vetoed packet never sees any evidence of an attack.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="TCP_ports">TCP ports</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: TCP ports"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_TCP_and_UDP_port_numbers" title="List of TCP and UDP port numbers">List of TCP and UDP port numbers</a></div>
<p>A TCP connection is identified by a four-<a href="/wiki/Tuple" title="Tuple">tuple</a> of the source address, source <a href="/wiki/Port_(computer_networking)" title="Port (computer networking)">port</a>, destination address, and destination port.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>d<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;92934.&#95;Glossary&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_92934._Glossary-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;80956&#95;87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_80956-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Port numbers are used to identify different services, and to allow multiple connections between hosts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;92932.2.&#95;Key&#95;TCP&#95;Concepts&#95;16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_92932.2._Key_TCP_Concepts-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> TCP uses <a href="/wiki/16-bit" class="mw-redirect" title="16-bit">16-bit</a> port numbers, providing 65,536 possible values for each of the source and destination ports.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;92933.1.&#95;Header&#95;Format&#95;19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_92933.1._Header_Format-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The dependency of connection identity on addresses means that TCP connections are bound to a single network path; TCP cannot use other routes that <a href="/wiki/Multihomed_host" class="mw-redirect" title="Multihomed host">multihomed hosts</a> have available, and connections break if an endpoint's address changes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPaaschBonaventure201451&#95;88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaaschBonaventure201451-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Port numbers are categorized into three basic categories: well-known, registered, and dynamic or private. The well-known ports are assigned by the <a href="/wiki/Internet_Assigned_Numbers_Authority" title="Internet Assigned Numbers Authority">Internet Assigned Numbers Authority</a> (IANA) and are typically used by system-level processes. Well-known applications running as servers and passively listening for connections typically use these ports. Some examples include: <a href="/wiki/FTP" class="mw-redirect" title="FTP">FTP</a> (20 and 21), <a href="/wiki/SSH" class="mw-redirect" title="SSH">SSH</a> (22), <a href="/wiki/TELNET" class="mw-redirect" title="TELNET">TELNET</a> (23), <a href="/wiki/SMTP" class="mw-redirect" title="SMTP">SMTP</a> (25), <a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">HTTP over SSL/TLS</a> (443), and <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a> (80).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>e<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Registered ports (1024–49151) may be assigned to specific services by third‑party developers, but some operating systems also allocate ephemeral client ports from this range. Dynamic or private ports (49152–65535) are not associated with any registered services and are commonly used exclusively as ephemeral ports for temporary client connections.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Network_Address_Translation" class="mw-redirect" title="Network Address Translation">Network Address Translation</a> (NAT), typically uses dynamic port numbers, on the public-facing side, to <a href="/wiki/Disambiguate" class="mw-redirect" title="Disambiguate">disambiguate</a> the flow of traffic that is passing between a public network and a private <a href="/wiki/Subnetwork" class="mw-redirect" title="Subnetwork">subnetwork</a>, thereby allowing many IP addresses (and their ports) on the subnet to be serviced by a single public-facing address.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Development">Development</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>TCP is a complex protocol. However, while significant enhancements have been made and proposed over the years, its most basic operation has not changed significantly since its first specification <a href="#CITEREFRFC_675">RFC 675</a> in 1974, and the v4 specification <a href="#CITEREFRFC_793">RFC 793</a>, published in September 1981. <a href="#CITEREFRFC_1122">RFC 1122</a>, published in October 1989, clarified a number of TCP protocol implementation requirements. A list of the 8 required specifications and over 20 strongly encouraged enhancements is available in <a href="#CITEREFRFC_7414">RFC 7414</a>. Among this list is <a href="#CITEREFRFC_2581">RFC 2581</a>, TCP Congestion Control, one of the most important TCP-related RFCs in recent years, describes updated algorithms that avoid undue congestion. In 2001, <a href="#CITEREFRFC_3168">RFC 3168</a> was written to describe <a href="/wiki/Explicit_Congestion_Notification" title="Explicit Congestion Notification">Explicit Congestion Notification</a> (ECN), a congestion avoidance signaling mechanism.
</p><p>The original TCP congestion avoidance algorithm was known as <i>TCP Tahoe</i>, but many alternative algorithms have since been proposed (including <a href="/wiki/TCP_Reno" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP Reno">TCP Reno</a>, <a href="/wiki/TCP_Vegas" title="TCP Vegas">TCP Vegas</a>, <a href="/wiki/FAST_TCP" title="FAST TCP">FAST TCP</a>, <a href="/wiki/TCP_New_Reno" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP New Reno">TCP New Reno</a>, and <a href="/wiki/TCP_Hybla" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP Hybla">TCP Hybla</a>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Multipath_TCP" title="Multipath TCP">Multipath TCP</a> (MPTCP)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;6182&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_6182-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;6824&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_6824-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is an ongoing effort within the IETF that aims at allowing a TCP connection to use multiple paths to maximize resource usage and increase redundancy. The redundancy offered by Multipath TCP in the context of wireless networks enables the simultaneous use of different networks, which brings higher throughput and better handover capabilities. Multipath TCP also brings performance benefits in datacenter environments.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The reference implementation<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> of Multipath TCP was developed in the Linux kernel.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Multipath TCP is used to support the Siri voice recognition application on iPhones, iPads and Macs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tcpcrypt" title="Tcpcrypt">tcpcrypt</a> is an extension proposed in July 2010 to provide transport-level encryption directly in TCP itself. It is designed to work transparently and not require any configuration. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">TLS</a> (SSL), tcpcrypt itself does not provide authentication, but provides simple primitives down to the application to do that. The tcpcrypt RFC was published by the IETF in May 2019.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/TCP_Fast_Open" title="TCP Fast Open">TCP Fast Open</a> is an extension to speed up the opening of successive TCP connections between two endpoints. It works by skipping the three-way handshake using a cryptographic <i>cookie</i>. It is similar to an earlier proposal called <a href="/wiki/T/TCP" title="T/TCP">T/TCP</a>, which was not widely adopted due to security issues.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-lwn&#95;97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lwn-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> TCP Fast Open was published as <a href="#CITEREFRFC_7413">RFC 7413</a> in 2014.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;7413&#95;98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_7413-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Proposed in May 2013, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Proportional_Rate_Reduction&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Proportional Rate Reduction (page does not exist)">Proportional Rate Reduction</a> (PRR) is a TCP extension developed by Google engineers. PRR ensures that the TCP window size after recovery is as close to the <a href="/wiki/TCP_congestion_control#Slow_start" title="TCP congestion control">slow start</a> threshold as possible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;6937&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_6937-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The algorithm is designed to improve the speed of recovery and is the default congestion control algorithm in Linux 3.2+ kernels.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Deprecated_proposals">Deprecated proposals</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Deprecated proposals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/TCP_Cookie_Transactions" title="TCP Cookie Transactions">TCP Cookie Transactions</a> (TCPCT) is an extension proposed in December 2009<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;6013&#95;101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_6013-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to secure servers against denial-of-service attacks. Unlike SYN cookies, TCPCT does not conflict with other TCP extensions such as <a href="/wiki/Window_scaling" class="mw-redirect" title="Window scaling">window scaling</a>. TCPCT was designed due to necessities of <a href="/wiki/DNSSEC" class="mw-redirect" title="DNSSEC">DNSSEC</a>, where servers have to handle large numbers of short-lived TCP connections. In 2016, TCPCT was <a href="/wiki/Deprecated" class="mw-redirect" title="Deprecated">deprecated</a> in favor of TCP Fast Open. The status of the original RFC was changed to <i>historic</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;7805&#95;102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_7805-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Hardware_implementations">Hardware implementations</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Hardware implementations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One way to overcome the processing power requirements of TCP is to build hardware implementations of it, widely known as <a href="/wiki/TCP_offload_engine" title="TCP offload engine">TCP offload engines</a> (TOE). The main problem of TOEs is that they are hard to integrate into computing systems, requiring extensive changes in the operating system of the computer or device.
</p><p>TCP low power (<em>TCPlp</em>) has been demonstrated to work in resource constrained environments where otherwise UDP-based <a href="/wiki/CoAP" class="mw-redirect" title="CoAP">CoAP</a> is preferred.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Wire_image_and_ossification">Wire image and ossification</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Wire image and ossification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wire_data" title="Wire data">wire data</a> of TCP provides significant information-gathering and modification opportunities to on-path observers, as the protocol metadata is transmitted in <a href="/wiki/Cleartext" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleartext">cleartext</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;85466&#95;105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_85466-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;85583&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_85583-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While this transparency is useful to network operators<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;90652.&#95;Current&#95;Uses&#95;of&#95;Transport&#95;Headers&#95;within&#95;the&#95;Network&#95;107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_90652._Current_Uses_of_Transport_Headers_within_the_Network-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and researchers,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;90653.&#95;Research,&#95;Development,&#95;and&#95;Deployment&#95;108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_90653._Research,_Development,_and_Deployment-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> information gathered from protocol metadata may reduce the end-user's privacy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;85588&#95;109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_85588-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This visibility and malleability of metadata has led to TCP being difficult to extend&#8212;a case of <a href="/wiki/Protocol_ossification" title="Protocol ossification">protocol ossification</a>&#8212;as any intermediate node (a '<a href="/wiki/Middlebox" title="Middlebox">middlebox</a>') can make decisions based on that metadata or even modify it,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;91702.3.&#95;Multi-party&#95;Interactions&#95;and&#95;Middleboxes&#95;110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_91702.3._Multi-party_Interactions_and_Middleboxes-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;9170A.5.&#95;TCP&#95;111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_9170A.5._TCP-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> breaking the <a href="/wiki/End-to-end_principle" title="End-to-end principle">end-to-end principle</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPapastergiouFairhurstRosBrunstrom2017620&#95;112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPapastergiouFairhurstRosBrunstrom2017620-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One measurement found that a third of paths across the Internet encounter at least one intermediary that modifies TCP metadata, and 6.5% of paths encounter harmful ossifying effects from intermediaries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEEdelineDonnet2019175–176&#95;113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdelineDonnet2019175–176-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Avoiding extensibility hazards from intermediaries placed significant constraints on the design of <a href="/wiki/MPTCP" class="mw-redirect" title="MPTCP">MPTCP</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERaiciuPaaschBarreFord20121&#95;114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaiciuPaaschBarreFord20121-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHesmansDuchenePaaschDetal20131&#95;115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHesmansDuchenePaaschDetal20131-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and difficulties caused by intermediaries have hindered the deployment of TCP Fast Open in <a href="/wiki/Web_browsers" class="mw-redirect" title="Web browsers">web browsers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERybczyńska2020&#95;116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERybczyńska2020-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another source of ossification is the difficulty of modification of TCP functions at the endpoints, typically in the <a href="/wiki/Operating_system_kernel" class="mw-redirect" title="Operating system kernel">operating system kernel</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPapastergiouFairhurstRosBrunstrom2017621&#95;117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPapastergiouFairhurstRosBrunstrom2017621-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or in hardware with a <a href="/wiki/TCP_offload_engine" title="TCP offload engine">TCP offload engine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECorbet2015&#95;118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorbet2015-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Performance">Performance</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Performance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As TCP provides applications with the abstraction of a <a href="/wiki/Reliable_byte_stream" title="Reliable byte stream">reliable byte stream</a>, it can suffer from <a href="/wiki/Head-of-line_blocking" title="Head-of-line blocking">head-of-line blocking</a>: if <a href="/wiki/Packet_reordering" class="mw-redirect" title="Packet reordering">packets are reordered</a> or <a href="/wiki/Packet_loss" title="Packet loss">lost</a> and need to be retransmitted (and thus are reordered), data from sequentially later parts of the stream may be received before sequentially earlier parts of the stream; however, the later data cannot typically be used until the earlier data has been received, incurring <a href="/wiki/Network_latency" class="mw-redirect" title="Network latency">network latency</a>. If multiple independent higher-level messages are <a href="/wiki/Encapsulation_(networking)" title="Encapsulation (networking)">encapsulated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time-division_multiplexing" title="Time-division multiplexing">multiplexed</a> onto a single TCP connection, then head-of-line blocking can cause processing of a fully-received message that was sent later to wait for delivery of a message that was sent earlier.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBriscoeBrunstromPetlundHayes201629–30&#95;119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriscoeBrunstromPetlundHayes201629–30-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Web_browsers" class="mw-redirect" title="Web browsers">Web browsers</a> attempt to mitigate head-of-line blocking by opening multiple parallel connections. This incurs the cost of connection establishment repeatedly, as well as multiplying the resources needed to track those connections at the endpoints.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMarx2020HOL&#95;blocking&#95;in&#95;HTTP/1.1&#95;120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx2020HOL_blocking_in_HTTP/1.1-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Parallel connections also have congestion control operating independently of each other, rather than being able to pool information together and respond more promptly to observed network conditions;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMarx2020Bonus:&#95;Transport&#95;Congestion&#95;Control&#95;121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx2020Bonus:_Transport_Congestion_Control-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> TCP's aggressive initial sending patterns can cause congestion if multiple parallel connections are opened; and the per-connection fairness model leads to a monopolization of resources by applications that take this approach.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIETF&#95;HTTP&#95;Working&#95;GroupWhy&#95;just&#95;one&#95;TCP&#95;connection?&#95;122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIETF_HTTP_Working_GroupWhy_just_one_TCP_connection?-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Connection establishment is a major contributor to latency as experienced by web users.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECorbet2018&#95;123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorbet2018-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;74133&#95;124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_74133-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> TCP's three-way handshake introduces one RTT of latency during connection establishment before data can be sent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;74133&#95;124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_74133-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For short flows, these delays are very significant.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESyMuellerBurkertFederrath2020271&#95;125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESyMuellerBurkertFederrath2020271-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security</a> (TLS) requires a handshake of its own for <a href="/wiki/Key_exchange" title="Key exchange">key exchange</a> at connection establishment. Because of the layered design, the TCP handshake and the TLS handshake proceed serially; the TLS handshake cannot begin until the TCP handshake has concluded.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEChenJeroJagielskiBoldyreva20218–9&#95;126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChenJeroJagielskiBoldyreva20218–9-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two RTTs are required for connection establishment with <a href="/wiki/TLS_1.2" class="mw-redirect" title="TLS 1.2">TLS 1.2</a> over TCP.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGhedini2018&#95;127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGhedini2018-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/TLS_1.3" class="mw-redirect" title="TLS 1.3">TLS 1.3</a> allows for zero RTT connection resumption in some circumstances, but, when layered over TCP, one RTT is still required for the TCP handshake, and this cannot assist the initial connection; zero RTT handshakes also present cryptographic challenges, as efficient, <a href="/wiki/Replay-safe" class="mw-redirect" title="Replay-safe">replay-safe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forward_secure" class="mw-redirect" title="Forward secure">forward secure</a> <a href="/wiki/Non-interactive_key_exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-interactive key exchange">non-interactive key exchange</a> is an open research topic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEChenJeroJagielskiBoldyreva20213–4&#95;128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChenJeroJagielskiBoldyreva20213–4-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> TCP Fast Open allows the transmission of data in the initial (i.e., SYN and SYN-ACK) packets, removing one RTT of latency during connection establishment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;74131&#95;129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_74131-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, TCP Fast Open has been difficult to deploy due to protocol ossification; as of 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, no <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">Web browsers</a> used it by default.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERybczyńska2020&#95;116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERybczyńska2020-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>TCP throughput is affected by <a href="/wiki/Packet_reordering" class="mw-redirect" title="Packet reordering">packet reordering</a>. Reordered packets can cause duplicate acknowledgments to be sent, which, if they cross a threshold, will then trigger a spurious retransmission and congestion control. Transmission behavior can also become bursty, as large ranges are acknowledged all at once when a reordered packet at the range's start is received (in a manner similar to how head-of-line blocking affects applications).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBlantonAllman20021–2&#95;130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlantonAllman20021–2-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="#CITEREFBlantonAllman2002">Blanton &amp; Allman (2002)</a> found that throughput was inversely related to the amount of reordering, up to a threshold where all reordering triggers spurious retransmission.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBlantonAllman20024–5&#95;131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlantonAllman20024–5-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mitigating reordering depends on a sender's ability to determine that it has sent a spurious retransmission, and hence on resolving retransmission ambiguity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBlantonAllman20023–4&#95;132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlantonAllman20023–4-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Reducing reordering-induced spurious retransmissions may slow recovery from genuine loss.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBlantonAllman20026–8&#95;133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlantonAllman20026–8-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Selective acknowledgment can provide a significant benefit to throughput; <a href="#CITEREFBruyeronHemonZhang1998">Bruyeron, Hemon &amp; Zhang (1998)</a> measured gains of up to 45%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBruyeronHemonZhang199867&#95;134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruyeronHemonZhang199867-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An important factor in the improvement is that selective acknowledgment can more often avoid going into slow start after a loss and can hence better use available bandwidth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBruyeronHemonZhang199872&#95;135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruyeronHemonZhang199872-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, TCP can only selectively acknowledge a maximum of three blocks of sequence numbers. This can limit the retransmission rate and hence loss recovery or cause needless retransmissions, especially in high-loss environments.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBhatRizkZink201714&#95;136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatRizkZink201714-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;90024.5.&#95;More&#95;ACK&#95;Ranges&#95;137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_90024.5._More_ACK_Ranges-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>TCP was originally designed for wired networks where packet loss is considered to be the result of <a href="/wiki/Network_congestion" title="Network congestion">network congestion</a> and the congestion window size is reduced dramatically as a precaution. However, wireless links are known to experience sporadic and usually temporary losses due to <a href="/wiki/Fading" title="Fading">fading</a>, shadowing, hand off, <a href="/wiki/Interference_(communication)" title="Interference (communication)">interference</a>, and other radio effects, that are not strictly congestion. After the (erroneous) back-off of the congestion window size, due to wireless packet loss, there may be a congestion avoidance phase with a conservative decrease in window size. This causes the radio link to be underused. Extensive research on combating these harmful effects has been conducted. Suggested solutions can be categorized as end-to-end solutions, which require modifications at the client or server,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Microsoft&#95;Academic&#95;Research&#95;138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Microsoft_Academic_Research-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> link layer solutions, such as <a href="/wiki/Radio_Link_Protocol" title="Radio Link Protocol">Radio Link Protocol</a> in cellular networks, or proxy-based solutions which require some changes in the network without modifying end nodes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Microsoft&#95;Academic&#95;Research&#95;138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Microsoft_Academic_Research-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-IEEE&#95;Computer&#95;Society&#95;139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE_Computer_Society-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A number of alternative congestion control algorithms, such as <a href="/wiki/TCP_Vegas" title="TCP Vegas">Vegas</a>, <a href="/wiki/TCP_Westwood" title="TCP Westwood">Westwood</a>, Veno, and Santa Cruz, have been proposed to help solve the wireless problem.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Acceleration">Acceleration</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Acceleration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/TCP_offload_engine" title="TCP offload engine">TCP offload engine</a>.</div>
<p>The idea of a TCP accelerator is to terminate TCP connections inside the network processor and then relay the data to a second connection toward the end system. The data packets that originate from the sender are buffered at the accelerator node, which is responsible for performing local retransmissions in the event of packet loss. Thus, in case of losses, the feedback loop between the sender and the receiver is shortened to the one between the acceleration node and the receiver which guarantees a faster delivery of data to the receiver.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Since TCP is a rate-adaptive protocol, the rate at which the TCP sender injects packets into the network is directly proportional to the prevailing load condition within the network as well as the processing capacity of the receiver. The prevalent conditions within the network are judged by the sender on the basis of the acknowledgments received by it. The acceleration node splits the feedback loop between the sender and the receiver and thus guarantees a shorter round trip time (RTT) per packet. A shorter RTT is beneficial as it ensures a quicker response time to any changes in the network and a faster adaptation by the sender to combat these changes.
</p><p>Disadvantages of the method include the fact that the TCP session has to be directed through the accelerator; this means that if routing changes so that the accelerator is no longer in the path, the connection will be broken. It also destroys the end-to-end property of the TCP ACK mechanism; when the ACK is received by the sender, the packet has been stored by the accelerator, not delivered to the receiver.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Debugging">Debugging</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Debugging"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Packet_sniffer" class="mw-redirect" title="Packet sniffer">packet sniffer</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Network_tap" title="Network tap">taps</a> TCP traffic on a network link, can be useful in debugging networks, network stacks, and applications that use TCP by showing an engineer what packets are passing through a link. Some networking stacks support the SO_DEBUG socket option, which can be enabled on the socket using setsockopt. That option dumps all the packets, TCP states, and events on that socket, which is helpful in debugging. <a href="/wiki/Netstat" title="Netstat">Netstat</a> is another utility that can be used for debugging.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Alternatives">Alternatives</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Alternatives"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>For many applications TCP is not appropriate. The application cannot normally access the packets coming after a lost packet until the retransmitted copy of the lost packet is received. This causes problems for real-time applications such as streaming media, real-time multiplayer games and <a href="/wiki/Voice_over_IP" title="Voice over IP">voice over IP</a> (VoIP) where it is generally more useful to get most of the data in a timely fashion than it is to get all of the data in order.
</p><p>For historical and performance reasons, most <a href="/wiki/Storage_area_network" title="Storage area network">storage area networks</a> (SANs) use <a href="/wiki/Fibre_Channel_Protocol" title="Fibre Channel Protocol">Fibre Channel Protocol</a> (FCP) over <a href="/wiki/Fibre_Channel" title="Fibre Channel">Fibre Channel</a> connections. For <a href="/wiki/Embedded_system" title="Embedded system">embedded systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Network_booting" title="Network booting">network booting</a>, and servers that serve simple requests from huge numbers of clients (e.g. <a href="/wiki/DNS" class="mw-redirect" title="DNS">DNS</a> servers) the complexity of TCP can be a problem. Tricks such as transmitting data between two hosts that are both behind <a href="/wiki/Network_address_translation" title="Network address translation">NAT</a> (using <a href="/wiki/STUN" title="STUN">STUN</a> or similar systems) are far simpler without a relatively complex protocol like TCP in the way.
</p><p>Generally, where TCP is unsuitable, the <a href="/wiki/User_Datagram_Protocol" title="User Datagram Protocol">User Datagram Protocol</a> (UDP) is used. This provides the same application <a href="/wiki/Multiplexing" title="Multiplexing">multiplexing</a> and checksums that TCP does, but does not handle streams or retransmission, giving the application developer the ability to code them in a way suitable for the situation, or to replace them with other methods such as <a href="/wiki/Forward_error_correction" class="mw-redirect" title="Forward error correction">forward error correction</a> or <a href="/wiki/Error_concealment" title="Error concealment">error concealment</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stream_Control_Transmission_Protocol" title="Stream Control Transmission Protocol">Stream Control Transmission Protocol</a> (SCTP) is another protocol that provides reliable stream-oriented services similar to TCP. It is newer and considerably more complex than TCP, and has not yet seen widespread deployment. However, it is especially designed to be used in situations where reliability and near-real-time considerations are important.
</p><p>Venturi Transport Protocol (VTP) is a patented <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_protocol" title="Proprietary protocol">proprietary protocol</a> that is designed to replace TCP transparently to overcome perceived inefficiencies related to wireless data transport. Especially in mobile communications and wireless networks, data transmission may become unstable due to high latency and signal interference.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/TCP_congestion_avoidance_algorithm" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP congestion avoidance algorithm">TCP congestion avoidance algorithm</a> works very well for ad-hoc environments where the data sender is not known in advance. If the environment is predictable, a timing-based protocol such as <a href="/wiki/Asynchronous_Transfer_Mode" title="Asynchronous Transfer Mode">Asynchronous Transfer Mode</a> (ATM) can avoid TCP's retransmission overhead.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/UDP-based_Data_Transfer_Protocol" title="UDP-based Data Transfer Protocol">UDP-based Data Transfer Protocol</a> (UDT) is designed for high-bandwidth, high-latency network environments, particularly for large-scale data transfers such as file or media streaming. In networks with a high  <a href="/wiki/Bandwidth-delay_product" title="Bandwidth-delay product">bandwidth-delay product</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> UDT offers advantages over TCP, providing better efficiency and fairness.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Multipurpose_Transaction_Protocol" title="Multipurpose Transaction Protocol">Multipurpose Transaction Protocol</a> (MTP/IP) is patented proprietary software that is designed to adaptively achieve high throughput and transaction performance in a wide variety of network conditions, particularly those where TCP is perceived to be inefficient.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Checksum_computation">Checksum computation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Checksum computation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Internet_checksum" title="Internet checksum">Internet checksum</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="TCP_checksum_for_IPv4">TCP checksum for IPv4</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: TCP checksum for IPv4"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>When TCP runs over <a href="/wiki/IPv4" title="IPv4">IPv4</a>, the method used to compute the checksum is defined as follows:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;9293&#95;18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_9293-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p><i>The checksum field is the 16-bit ones' complement of the ones' complement sum of all 16-bit words in the header and text. The checksum computation needs to ensure the 16-bit alignment of the data being summed. If a segment contains an odd number of header and text octets, alignment can be achieved by padding the last octet with zeros on its right to form a 16-bit word for checksum purposes. The pad is not transmitted as part of the segment. While computing the checksum, the checksum field itself is replaced with zeros.</i></p></blockquote>
<p>In other words, after appropriate padding, all 16-bit words are added using <a href="/wiki/End-around_carry" class="mw-redirect" title="End-around carry">ones' complement arithmetic</a>. The sum is then bitwise complemented and inserted as the checksum field. A pseudo-header that mimics the IPv4 packet header used in the checksum computation is as follows:
</p>

<p>The checksum is computed over the following fields:
</p>
<dl><dt>Source address&#58; 32 bits</dt>
<dd>The source address in the IPv4 header</dd>
<dt>Destination address&#58; 32 bits</dt>
<dd>The destination address in the IPv4 header</dd>
<dt>Zeroes&#58; 8 bits</dt>
<dd>All zeroes</dd>
<dt>Protocol&#58; 8 bits</dt>
<dd>The protocol value for TCP: <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000600000000000000♠"></span>6</span></dd>
<dt>TCP length&#58; 16 bits</dt>
<dd>The length of the TCP header and data (measured in octets). For example, let's say we have IPv4 packet with Total Length of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002200000000000000♠"></span>200&#160;bytes</span> and IHL value of 5, which indicates a length of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000500000000000000♠"></span>5&#160;bits × 32&#160;bits</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002160000000000000♠"></span>160&#160;bits</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001200000000000000♠"></span>20&#160;bytes</span>. We can compute the TCP length as <span class="nowrap">(Total Length) &#8722; (IPv4 Header Length)</span> i.e. <span class="nowrap">200 &#8722; 20</span>, which results in <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002180000000000000♠"></span>180&#160;bytes</span>.</dd></dl>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="TCP_checksum_for_IPv6">TCP checksum for IPv6</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: TCP checksum for IPv6"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>When TCP runs over <a href="/wiki/IPv6" title="IPv6">IPv6</a>, the method used to compute the checksum is changed:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;8200&#95;142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERFC_8200-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p><i>Any transport or other upper-layer protocol that includes the addresses from the IP header in its checksum computation must be modified for use over IPv6, to include the 128-bit IPv6 addresses instead of 32-bit IPv4 addresses.</i>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>A pseudo-header that mimics the IPv6 header for computation of the checksum is shown below.
</p>

<p>The checksum is computed over the following fields:
</p>
<dl><dt>Source address&#58; 128 bits</dt>
<dd>The address in the IPv6 header.</dd>
<dt>Destination address&#58; 128 bits</dt>
<dd>The final destination; if the IPv6 packet doesn't contain a Routing header, TCP uses the destination address in the IPv6 header, otherwise, at the originating node, it uses the address in the last element of the Routing header, and, at the receiving node, it uses the destination address in the IPv6 header.</dd>
<dt>TCP length&#58; 32 bits</dt>
<dd>The length of the TCP header and data (measured in octets).</dd>
<dt>Zeroes&#58; 24 bits&#59; <code>Zeroes == 0</code></dt>
<dd>All zeroes.</dd>
<dt>Next header&#58; 8 bits</dt>
<dd>The protocol value for TCP: <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000600000000000000♠"></span>6</span>.</dd></dl>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Checksum_offload">Checksum offload <span class="anchor" id="checksum&#95;offload"></span></h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transmission_Control_Protocol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Checksum offload"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many TCP/IP software stack implementations provide options to use hardware assistance to automatically compute the checksum in the <a href="/wiki/Network_adapter" class="mw-redirect" title="Network adapter">network adapter</a> prior to transmission onto the network or upon reception from the network for validation. This may reduce CPU load associated with calculating the checksum, potentially increasing overall network performance.
</p><p>This feature may cause <a href="/wiki/Packet_analyzer" title="Packet analyzer">packet analyzers</a> that are unaware or uncertain about the use of checksum offload to report invalid checksums in outbound packets that have not yet reached the network adapter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This will only occur for packets that are intercepted before being transmitted by the network adapter; all packets transmitted by the network adaptor on the wire will have valid checksums.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This issue can also occur when monitoring packets being transmitted between virtual machines on the same host, where a virtual device driver may omit the checksum calculation (as an optimization), knowing that the checksum will be calculated later by the VM host kernel or its physical hardware.
</p>

</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite&#95;note-xgruR-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-xgruR_7-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFPostel1977" class="citation cs2"><a href="/wiki/Jon_Postel" title="Jon Postel">Postel, Jon</a> (15 August 1977), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/ien/ien2.txt"><i>Comments on Internet Protocol and TCP</i></a>, IEN 2, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190516055704/http://www.rfc-editor.org/ien/ien2.txt">archived</a> from the original on May 16, 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 11,</span> 2016</span>, <q>We are screwing up in our design of internet protocols by violating the principle of layering. Specifically we are trying to use TCP to do two things: serve as a host level end to end protocol, and to serve as an internet packaging and routing protocol. These two things should be provided in a layered and modular way.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Comments+on+Internet+Protocol+and+TCP&amp;rft.date=1977-08-15&amp;rft.aulast=Postel&amp;rft.aufirst=Jon&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.rfc-editor.org%2Fien%2Fien2.txt&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATransmission+Control+Protocol" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-140">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20240422182258/https://www.frame.ie/use-cases/understanding-tcp-and-the-need-for-tcp-acceleration/">"TCP Acceleration"</a>. <li id="cite&#95;note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFYunhong&#95;GuXinwei&#95;HongRobert&#95;L.&#95;Grossman2004" class="citation web cs1">Yunhong Gu; Xinwei Hong; Robert L. Grossman (2004). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://udt.sourceforge.net/doc/gridnet-v8.pdf">"An Analysis of AIMD Algorithm with Decreasing Increases"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160305003043/http://udt.sourceforge.net/doc/gridnet-v8.pdf">Archived</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> from the original on 2016-03-05.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=An+Analysis+of+AIMD+Algorithm+with+Decreasing+Increases&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.au=Yunhong+Gu&amp;rft.au=Xinwei+Hong&amp;rft.au=Robert+L.+Grossman&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fudt.sourceforge.net%2Fdoc%2Fgridnet-v8.pdf&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATransmission+Control+Protocol" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-FOOTNOTE&#82;FC&#95;8200-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTERFC_8200_142-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRFC_8200">RFC 8200</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://wiki.wireshark.org/CaptureSetup/Offloading">"Wireshark: Offloading"</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170131220028/https://wiki.wireshark.org/CaptureSetup/Offloading/">Archived</a> from the original on 2017-01-31<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2017-02-24</span></span>. <q>Wireshark captures packets before they are sent to the network adapter. It won't see the correct checksum because it has not been calculated yet. Even worse, most OSes don't bother initialize this data so you're probably seeing little chunks of memory that you shouldn't. New installations of Wireshark 1.2 and above disable IP, TCP, and UDP checksum validation by default. You can disable checksum validation in each of those dissectors by hand if needed.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Wireshark%3A+Offloading&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fwiki.wireshark.org%2FCaptureSetup%2FOffloading&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATransmission+Control+Protocol" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.wireshark.org/docs/wsug_html_chunked/ChAdvChecksums.html">"Wireshark: Checksums"</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20161022133751/https://www.wireshark.org/docs/wsug_html_chunked/ChAdvChecksums.html">Archived</a> from the original on 2016-10-22<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2017-02-24</span></span>. <q>Checksum offloading often causes confusion as the network packets to be transmitted are handed over to Wireshark before the checksums are actually calculated. Wireshark gets these "empty" checksums and displays them as invalid, even though the packets will contain valid checksums when they leave the network hardware later.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Wireshark%3A+Checksums&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.wireshark.org%2Fdocs%2Fwsug&#95;html&#95;chunked%2FChAdvChecksums.html&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATransmission+Control+Protocol" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
